<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="27a2590111ab4d13a7b2848c76e7f72a"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="27a2590111ab4d13a7b2848c76e7f72a">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'dbbca4e9-2212-40de-9925-b1080c29ac2d'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d49995839" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>With the addition of FEC, a robust electrode-electrolyte interphase formed on the surface of Sb anode and graphite cathode, inhibiting the pulverization of Sb and the expansion of graphite. The formation of FEC-rich anions solvation structure facilitates the insertion of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>, which significantly improves the reversible capacity. Thus, guaranteeing the greatly enhanced performance of high voltage and power density Sb||graphite dual-ion batteries.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ea2cd001-4a4e-4706-a015-f0cf9a7a4f64/anie202313142-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/ea2cd001-4a4e-4706-a015-f0cf9a7a4f64/anie202313142-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/766c814f-7de9-45a0-b8e7-992c58339f1f/anie202313142-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ea2cd001-4a4e-4706-a015-f0cf9a7a4f64/anie202313142-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d49995848" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium dual-ion batteries (Na-DIBs) have attracted increasing attention due to their high operative voltages and low-cost raw materials. However, the practical applications of Na-DIBs are still hindered by the issues, such as low capacity and poor Coulombic efficiency, which is highly correlated with the compatibility between electrode and electrolyte but rarely investigated. Herein, fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) is introduced into the electrolyte to regulate cation/anion solvation structure and the stability of cathode/anode-electrolyte interphase of Na-DIBs. The FEC modulates the environment of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> solvation sheath and facilitates the interaction of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> on graphite. In addition, the NaF-rich interphase caused by the preferential decomposition of FEC effectively inhibits side reactions and pulverization of anodes with the electrolyte. Consequently, Sb||graphite full cells in FEC-containing electrolyte achieve an improved capacity, cycling stability and Coulombic efficiency. This work elucidates the underlying mechanism of bifunctional FEC and provides an alternative strategy of building high-performance dual ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202313142-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202313142-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The rising need for energy storage devices that comply with environmental requirements has spurred the creation of alternative options to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs).<span><sup><a href="#anie202313142-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202313142-bib-0001_R_d49995830e592" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> Among various innovative alternative devices, sodium-based battery technologies possess the merits of long cycle life, natural abundance of sodium source, and similar electrochemical properties to LIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202313142-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202313142-bib-0005_R_d49995830e595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat. Commun.">2</a></sup></span> Therefore, rechargeable energy storage devices based on sodium electrochemistry are expected as promising candidates to regulate the output of large-scale power grids that apply intermittent solar and wind energy.<span><sup><a href="#anie202313142-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202313142-bib-0006_R_d49995830e598" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3">3</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Recently, as one of the most important branches for sodium-based battery technologies, sodium dual-ion batteries (Na-DIBs) have attracted extensive attention due to their high energy density, high operating voltage, environmental benignity and low cost.<span><sup><a href="#anie202313142-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202313142-bib-0009_R_d49995830e603" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4">4</a></sup></span> Unlike conventional “rocking chair” batteries, both Na<sup>+</sup> and anions in the Na-DIBs electrolyte participate in the energy conversion process. During charge/discharge process, the cations and anions in electrolytes are reversibly stored/released into anode and cathode, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#anie202313142-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202313142-bib-0014_R_d49995830e608" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5">5</a></sup></span> Therefore, the electrolyte not only provides the basic operating environment of batteries, but also affects the electrochemical performance of Na-DIBs, such as cycling stability, voltage plateau, Coulombic efficiency, and even the reaction mechanism.<span><sup><a href="#anie202313142-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202313142-bib-0018_R_d49995830e611" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6">6</a></sup></span> Specifically, the electrolyte directly determines the formation of electrode-electrolyte interphase (EEI) and the transfer process of cations and anions between liquid and solid phases.<span><sup><a href="#anie202313142-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202313142-bib-0022_R_d49995830e614" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Engl.">7</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Until now, the research on electrolyte of dual ion batteries is still in the primary stage and several strategies such as highly concentrated electrolytes (HCEs),<span><sup><a href="#anie202313142-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202313142-bib-0023_R_d49995830e619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8">8</a></sup></span> ionic liquid electrolytes (ILs),<span><sup><a href="#anie202313142-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202313142-bib-0026_R_d49995830e622" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nat. Commun.">9</a></sup></span> gel polymer electrolytes (GPEs)<span><sup><a href="#anie202313142-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202313142-bib-0027_R_d49995830e625" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10">10</a></sup></span> and fluorinated electrolyte<span><sup><a href="#anie202313142-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202313142-bib-0030_R_d49995830e628" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a></sup></span> have been proposed. However, HCEs and ILs are not cost-effective, and the high viscosity of these electrolytes, leads to under-utilization of the full battery performance. Meanwhile, the application of GPEs is limited by their poor ionic conductivity. On the other hand, carbonates such as ethylene carbonate (EC) and propylene carbonate (PC) process excellent compatibility with various electrodes, which are the most common and successful commercial electrolyte solvents.<span><sup><a href="#anie202313142-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202313142-bib-0031_R_d49995830e631" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a></sup></span> Nevertheless, their application in Na-DIBs is still frustrated due to the low oxidation potential, co-intercalation of anions and solvents, and insufficient passivation ability.<span><sup><a href="#anie202313142-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202313142-bib-0019_R_d49995830e635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6a - Mater. Today Sustainability">6a</a></sup></span> Therefore, the development of low-cost electrolytes with reliable physicochemical properties that can form robust EEI and facilitate stable cation/anion de-intercalation is essential for the future commercialization of Na-DIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202313142-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202313142-bib-0032_R_d49995830e638" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">13</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>One simple and effective approach to improving electrochemical performance through electrolyte engineering is the use of electrolyte additives.<span><sup><a href="#anie202313142-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202313142-bib-0033_R_d49995830e643" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - ACS Energy Lett.">14</a></sup></span> For instance, benefiting from the favorable F-rich reduction product and excellent antioxidation ability, FEC has been widely applied as an EEI-film formation additive for next-generation batteries.<span><sup><a href="#anie202313142-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202313142-bib-0034_R_d49995830e646" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">15</a></sup></span> Nevertheless, few efforts have been made to investigate the effects of fluorinated carbonates on the stability and the corresponding solvation behavior of carriers in Na-DIBs. It remains uncertain whether the beneficial effects of FEC on the anode or cathode can be transferred to Na-DIBs.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, with Sb and graphite as anode and cathode respectively, we study the electrochemical performance of Na-DIB in the electrolyte containing FEC additive. Compared to FEC-free electrolyte (1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> salt in PC solvent), we found that the addition of FEC additive (5 wt %) promoted the formation of an inorganic NaF-rich EEI on both cathode and anode, as well as changed the solvation behavior of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions. This modification not only effectively suppresses the excessive decomposition of NaPF<sub>6</sub> salt and PC solvent, but also facilitates the intercalation of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions into graphite cathode. As a result, the designed Sb||graphite full cell achieves a high discharge specific capacity of 58.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 93.1 % after 50 cycles (compared to 48.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 79.7 % for baseline electrolyte). In addition, the cell shows an energy density of 194.95 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (based on the mass of active materials of both cathode and anode) at a power density of 157.78 W kg<sup>−1</sup>. This work provides a simple additive engineering for high performance Sb||graphite Na-DIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202313142-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202313142-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>To demonstrate the superiority of FEC on electrochemical performance, the cycling stability and rate performance of Sb anode and graphite cathode with 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>-PC and 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>-PC+5 % FEC electrolyte were compared by using Sb||Na and graphite||Na half cells, respectively. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0001">1a</a>, the Sb anode delivers a high discharge specific capacity of 517.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 514.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (second cycle) with 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>-PC and 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>-PC+5 % FEC electrolyte at 1 C, respectively. While the Sb||Na cell with 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>-PC electrolyte shows a severe capacity decay after 10 cycles, with a capacity retention of almost 0 % after 150 cycles. This may be attributed to the fragile solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI) formed on the surface of Sb anode, which is continuously destroyed by the volume change of Sb particles during the charging and discharging process, leading to severe electrolyte decomposition due to the continuous creation of fresh surfaces and reconstruction of new SEI films. In contrast, the Sb||Na cell exhibits a reversible discharge capacity of 346.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 68 % (based on the second discharge capacity) after 150 cycles. Correspondingly, Sb||Na cell in 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>-PC+5 % FEC electrolyte also show a better high-rate performance than that of 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>-PC electrolyte (Figure <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0001">1b</a>, <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0001">c</a> and S1a). The cell using 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>-PC+5 % FEC electrolyte provides 90.5 % capacity retention at 2 C in comparison with 0.2 C. The superior cycling and rate performance of Sb anode in 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>-PC+5 % FEC electrolyte can be attributed to the regulatory effect on the composition of both organic and inorganic constituents during the formation of SEI. This regulation achieves a harmonious equilibrium between the rigidity and ductility afforded by the SEI structure, ultimately leading to enhanced cycling stability of the Sb anodes. Figure <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0001">1d</a> shows the cycling performance of graphite||Na cell with 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>-PC and 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>-PC+5 % FEC electrolyte. The graphite cathode with 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>-PC electrolyte exhibits faster capacity decay, with a capacity retention of 76.5 % at 100 cycles, while the cell with electrolyte containing FEC has no obvious attenuation within the same number of cycles. Meanwhile, the graphite||Na cell with 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>-PC+5 % FEC electrolyte shows a slightly better high-rate capability than that with 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>-PC (Figure <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0001">1e</a>, <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0001">f</a> and S1b). In summary, both Sb anode and graphite cathode with FEC-contained electrolyte show longer cycling life and higher rate capability than those with FEC-free electrolyte.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202313142-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d15283b8-f4cf-4577-ac28-ac5bc9697b8c/anie202313142-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d15283b8-f4cf-4577-ac28-ac5bc9697b8c/anie202313142-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ea5c5e27-7c69-40bd-9524-2aafa25bc93a/anie202313142-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d15283b8-f4cf-4577-ac28-ac5bc9697b8c/anie202313142-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202313142-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202313142" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Electrochemical performance of Sb||Na and graphite||Na half cells. (a) Cycling performance and (b) rate performance of Sb||Na cell in 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC and 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC+5 % FEC electrolyte. (c) Corresponding charge–discharge curves of Sb||Na cell at various current densities in 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC+5 % FEC. (d) Cycling performance and (e) rate performance of graphite||Na cell in 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC and 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC+5 % FEC electrolyte. (f) Corresponding charge–discharge curves of graphite||Na cell at various current densities in 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC+5 % FEC.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In general, the electrochemical performance is highly associated with the anion-solvation interaction. To better understand the effect of FEC on the solvation structure of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anion, Raman, Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectroscopy and nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR), measurements were performed. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0002">2a</a> and Figure S2, Raman spectra show that the C−O peak of FEC at 731 cm<sup>−1</sup> and solvated PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> peak at 742 cm<sup>−1</sup> gets stronger with the addition of FEC, indicating that the increasing ratio of solvated PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> after the addition of FEC. This result is also confirmed by FTIR spectra (Figure S3), suggesting an enhanced solvation structure of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions after the addition of FEC. Subsequently, we further investigate the changes in electron density around F atom of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> before and after adding FEC. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0002">2b</a> and Figure S4, an obvious upfield and downfield shift can be observed from the <sup>19</sup>F NMR peak of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions and <sup>1</sup>H NMR peak of FEC, respectively, indicating a significantly increased electron density around the F atom of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions and decreased electron density around the H atom of FEC molecule. These results demonstrate that PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anion is endowed with strong enhanced anion-solvent interaction with FEC molecules.<span><sup><a href="#anie202313142-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202313142-bib-0035_R_d49995830e816" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - ACS Energy Lett.">16</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202313142-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8591ba56-02de-41bb-b15d-6e5661b89a35/anie202313142-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/8591ba56-02de-41bb-b15d-6e5661b89a35/anie202313142-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/71162a25-9fbf-4bf2-b7e3-641313c6f14d/anie202313142-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8591ba56-02de-41bb-b15d-6e5661b89a35/anie202313142-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202313142-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202313142" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Experimental and theoretical analysis of solvation structure. (a) Raman spectra of 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC and 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC+5 % FEC electrolyte. (b) Natural-abundance <sup>19</sup>F NMR spectra of different components and electrolytes. RDF and coordination number of (c) F−H pairs and (d) Na<sup>+</sup>−O pairs in 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC+5 % FEC electrolyte. (e) AIMD simulation snapshot of 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC+5 % FEC electrolyte. Representative solvation structures of (f) Na<sup>+</sup> cations and (g) PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In addition, ab initio molecular dynamics (AIMD) simulations (Figure <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0002">2c</a>–<a href="#anie202313142-fig-0002">g</a>, S5 and S6) were carried out to further understand the difference of the solvation structure of 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>-PC and 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>-PC+5 % FEC electrolyte. The snapshots of these solvation structures of Na<sup>+</sup> and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> ions in 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>-PC and 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>-PC+5 % FEC electrolyte from AIMD simulations are shown in Figure <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0002">2e</a>–<a href="#anie202313142-fig-0002">g</a> and Figure S6. The PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions can be closely coordinated with FEC, which shows the higher binding energies of −3.182 kcal/mol between PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> and FEC than that of PC (−2.556 kcal/mol) (Figure S7). As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0002">2c</a>, the radial distribution functions (RDFs) of Na<sup>+</sup>-carbonyl oxygen (Na<sup>+</sup>−O) pairs reveal that FEC participates in the first solvation shell of Na<sup>+</sup> cations and the peak of Na<sup>+</sup>−O (FEC) pair contribution is estimated to be 0.23 nm. No major difference of Na<sup>+</sup>−O peak of PC is observed in two different electrolytes, indicating the addition of FEC has little effect on the solvation behavior of Na<sup>+</sup> by PC. In Figure <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0002">2d</a>, a sharp peak of the F(PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>)−H(FEC) pairs is identified at 0.2 nm in 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC+5 % FEC electrolyte, implying the PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions are preferentially coordinated with the FEC than PC molecules. While the PC molecules are farthest from PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions with the primary F(PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>)−H(PC) peak of 0.25 nm. Such tendency is in accord with characterization results corroborating the effect of FEC on the solvation structure of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anion.</p>
         
         <p>Post-mortem characterizations were then carried out to unveil the interfacial characteristics of the Sb anode and graphite cathode after adding FEC additive in electrolyte. With the information gained through in-depth X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS), we are able to investigate the internal structure and chemical component of the SEI and cathode-electrolyte interphase (CEI) on the cycled Sb and graphite electrode. According to the XPS depth profiles on Sb anode with 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC electrolyte (Figure <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0003">3a</a> and Figure S8), the intensity of NaF peak in F 1s spectra increases from the outer to inner layer gradually, but accompanied by the high intensity of P−F signal throughout the whole Ar<sup>+</sup> sputtering process. Meanwhile, the signal of Na<sub>x</sub>PO<sub>y</sub>F<sub>z</sub> species in P 2p spectra show a significant increase during the sputtering depth from 20 s to 60 s, which indicates that the severe decomposition of NaPF<sub>6</sub> in FEC-free electrolyte. In addition, in Na 1s spectra (Figure <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0003">3a</a>), it can be observed that ROCO<sub>2</sub>Na is the main species during the whole sputtering process, suggesting the severe decomposition of PC solvents and the generation of organic Na-rich species. In contrast, both the spectra of F 1s and Na 1s exhibit strong NaF peak in 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC+5 % FEC electrolyte, while only weak P−F and Na<sub>x</sub>PO<sub>y</sub>F<sub>z</sub> signal can be observed in F 1s and P 2p spectra, which indicates the PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> decomposition can be effectively suppressed (Figure <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0003">3c</a>). In addition, Na 1s spectra suggest that the main component change from ROCO<sub>2</sub>Na to NaF after adding FEC additive, attributing to the prior decomposition of FEC on the Sb anode surface due to the lower lowest unoccupied molecular orbital (LUMO) energy of FEC and Na<sup>+</sup>−FEC (Figure S10). Significantly, a lower C, O and P ratio but higher F and Na ratio can be seen upon the addition of FEC (Figure <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0003">3e</a>), confirming the inhibition of the severe decomposition of PC solvents and NaPF<sub>6</sub> salt.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202313142-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4d1c3507-52fa-4765-918c-e2f65365b28b/anie202313142-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4d1c3507-52fa-4765-918c-e2f65365b28b/anie202313142-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4e30fdbc-3b59-4655-82e9-9d39523d4899/anie202313142-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4d1c3507-52fa-4765-918c-e2f65365b28b/anie202313142-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202313142-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202313142" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>XPS characterization of the EEI components on cycled Sb anode and graphite cathode. F 1s, Na 1s and P 2p XPS spectra of the cycled Sb anode after 20 cycles in (a)1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC and (c) 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC+5 % FEC electrolyte. F 1s, Na 1s and P 2p XPS spectra of the cycled graphite cathode after 20 cycles in (b)1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC and (d) 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC+5 % FEC electrolyte. The depth-dependent elemental concentration on (e) Sb anode and (f) graphite cathode in1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC and 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC+5 % FEC electrolyte.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Such an effect of FEC on Sb anode is quite different for graphite cathode. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0003">3b</a> and Figure S9, the poor intensity of NaF and P−F peaks can be detected on graphite surface with 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC electrolyte, while the surface is instead dominated by the strong intensity of C−O, O−C=O and C=O signals, implying the severe oxidative decomposition of PC solvents.<span><sup><a href="#anie202313142-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202313142-bib-0036_R_d49995830e1028" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Power Sources">17</a></sup></span> In contrast, the F 1s spectra with 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC+5 %FEC electrolyte show a significantly strong P−F and NaF peaks throughout the whole sputtering process (Figure <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0003">3d</a>). As the sputtering depth increases, the NaF in both F 1s and Na 1s spectra are gradually enriched, which indicates that adding FEC can also lead to NaF-rich CEI on the surface of graphite cathode. This may be attributed to the higher highest occupied molecular high orbital (HOMO) energy levels of PF<sub>6</sub>−FEC complex than the individual FEC molecule caused by the proton-transfer reactions between PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> and FEC (Figure S10). During the first discharge process, the graphite cathode undergoes a reduction process, where electrons are accepted from the electrolyte and occupy the LUMO. At a certain voltage, a large amount of NaF produced by FEC derivatives will also be observed on the graphite cathode.<span><sup><a href="#anie202313142-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202313142-bib-0037_R_d49995830e1042" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">18</a></sup></span> From the P 2p spectra, it can be found that there is a strong PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> peak on the graphite surface after the addition of FEC, but gradually decreases with the increase of sputtering time, which indicates that the FEC facilitates the insertion of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions in the graphite, resulting in a large number of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions remaining in the graphite interlayer. The atomic ratio of elements in graphite cathode reveals that the CEI originating from 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC+5 % FEC electrolyte is characterized by a distinctly high F and Na content but low C and O content (Figure <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0003">3f</a>). Thus, it can be deduced that both SEI and CEI formed with FEC are enriched with inorganic NaF-rich components, which can passivate the Sb anode and graphite cathode, inhibit severe electrolyte decomposition and facilitate the insertion of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions in the graphite cathode.</p>
         
         <p>The scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and high-resolution transmission electron microscope (HRTEM) were further performed to study the compositional and morphological evolution of the SEI and CEI layers. As shown in Figure S11a, b, the Sb anode interface in the FEC-free electrolyte shows the apparent cracking, while the dense and intact morphology was observed with 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC+5 % FEC electrolyte. More detailed differences can be detected through HRTEM. In 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC electrolyte, a nonuniform amorphous layer with a thickness of 5–13.4 nm is covered on the Sb anode. The inorganic region was identified as Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> as the magnified lattice spacing of 3.71 and 3.35 Å is well-matched with the (101) and (002) crystal plane of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (Figure <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0004">4a</a>, <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0004">e</a>, and <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0004">i</a>). In sharp contrast, a dense and uniform SEI layer with a thickness of 3.5 nm is observed in Figure <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0004">4b</a>. The fast Fourier transform (FFT) characterization displays that the magnified lattice spacing of 2.31 Å is well-matched with the (200) crystal plane of NaF, which further confirms the results from XPS (Figure <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0004">4f</a>, <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0004">j</a>). For the cathode side, the SEM images show that the graphite interface is smoother in 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC+5 % FEC electrolyte than that of 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC electrolyte (Figure S11c, d). HRTEM images exhibit that the graphite cathodes cycled in FEC-free electrolyte are coated by amorphous CEI with a thickness of 7.2 nm (Figure <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0004">4c</a>). Almost no crystalline lattices can be found in the layered structure, except for the lattices of graphite with a spacing of 3.35 Å (Figure <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0004">4g</a>, <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0004">k</a>). Additionally, the expansion of graphitic layers can be observed (Figure S12 and S13), possibly due to the presence of residual anions that are constrained within the graphite structure by the thick and rough CEI in 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC electrolyte. As comparison, a highly crystalline CEI layer with a uniform thickness of 3.7 nm is found on the graphite surface when cycled in the 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC+5 % FEC electrolyte (Figure <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0004">4d</a>). As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0004">4h</a>, <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0004">l</a>, the highly crystalline CEI layer is comprised of clear lattices of NaF resting on the top of graphite surface, which is highly consistent with XPS analysis. Thus, the comparison of the characteristics concerning the 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC and 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC+5 % FEC electrolyte can be found in Figure <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0004">4m</a>, <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0004">n</a>. It can be summarized that a thinner and highly crystalline NaF-rich SEI and CEI layer formed on the Sb and graphite surface, respectively, which are mainly derived from the decomposition of FEC additive. In contrast, the CEI and SEI formed with 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC electrolyte are enriched with organic components, which cannot passivate the electrode, leading to severe electrolyte (both salt and solvent) decomposition and electrode cracking.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202313142-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8b9c7aef-f6b6-486f-b612-136b5b0c67ea/anie202313142-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/8b9c7aef-f6b6-486f-b612-136b5b0c67ea/anie202313142-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/dc62d351-2951-4e69-ad1b-ed148e285265/anie202313142-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8b9c7aef-f6b6-486f-b612-136b5b0c67ea/anie202313142-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202313142-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202313142" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>HRTEM characterization of the EEI components on cycled Sb anode and graphite cathode. The SEI structure and corresponding FFT of SEI on cycled Sb anode in (a, e, and i) 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC and (b, f, and j) 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC+5 % FEC electrolyte. The CEI structure and corresponding FFT of CEI on cycled graphite cathode in (c, g, and k) 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC and (d, h, and i) 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC+5 % FEC electrolyte. The properties of (m) 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC+5 % FEC and (n) 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC electrolyte</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In addition, the phase evolution process of Sb anode and graphite cathode was investigated by in situ XRD to verify the compatibility of 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC+5 % FEC electrolyte. The charge/discharge curves and in situ XRD patterns of Sb anode are shown in Figure <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0005">5a</a>. During the discharge process, the characteristic peak of Sb gradually weakened and disappeared when discharged to ≈0.2 V. Meanwhile, the characteristic peaks of Na<sub>3</sub>Sb appeared after discharged to ≈0.4 V and the peaks intensity gradually increased until full discharge. In the subsequent charging process, the peak intensity of Na<sub>3</sub>Sb attenuated gradually and disappeared when charged to 0.9 V. Noticeably, the characteristic peak of Sb did not emerge even charged to 1.5 V, which indicates that the Sb loses its crystallinity, and exists in nanocrystalline or amorphous state. In the second discharge process, the peaks of Na<sub>3</sub>Sb appeared again when discharged to ≈0.4 V, which is similar to the first discharge.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202313142-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/498833f0-a78a-400f-b46d-0fd919389f8e/anie202313142-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/498833f0-a78a-400f-b46d-0fd919389f8e/anie202313142-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3300e0f5-e20e-4232-99a6-83c1f0781de0/anie202313142-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/498833f0-a78a-400f-b46d-0fd919389f8e/anie202313142-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202313142-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202313142" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Structural evolution of electrode materials in 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC+5 % FEC electrolyte. Charge/discharge curve, in situ XRD patterns and the corresponding contour plot of (a) Sb anode and (b) graphite cathode.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0005">5b</a> shows the charge/discharge curves and in situ XRD patterns of graphite cathode. During the process of discharging, the intensity of the characteristic (002) peak of graphite gradually weakens and then moves to a lower angle. In addition, due to the successive PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> intercalation, the two new peaks at around 23.16° and 29.54° appeared when charged to ≈4.5 V and shifted to lower and higher angles during the subsequent charging process, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#anie202313142-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202313142-bib-0035_R_d49995830e1223" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - ACS Energy Lett.">16</a></sup></span> The three peaks shift to 20.50°, 25.74° and 30.98° after being charged to 5.0 V. In the subsequent charging process, the peak change is highly reversible, and only the characteristic (002) peak of graphite exists after discharge to 3.0 V.</p>
         
         <p>The Sb||graphite full cell was finally assembled to evaluate the feasibility of the 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC+5 % FEC electrolyte (Figure <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0006">6a</a>). During the charging process, Na<sup>+</sup> ions migrate from the electrolyte to the Sb anode while PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions reversibly intercalate from electrolyte to the graphite cathode. Correspondingly, the Na<sup>+</sup> and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> ions are simultaneously released from Sb anode and graphite cathode, respectively, and return to the electrolyte during the discharge process. The cycling stability of the Sb||graphite full cell with two electrolytes was compared in Figure <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0006">6b</a>. The cell with 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC+5 % FEC electrolyte exhibits a capacity of 53.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 50 cycles (Figure S14), which is much better than that in FEC-free electrolyte. For comparison with other electrochemical energy storage devices and competitive sodium-ion batteries, the Ragone plot was shown in Figure S15 and S16. As a result, the Sb||graphite full cell shows an energy density of 194.95 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (based on the mass of active materials of both cathode and anode) at a power density of 157.78 W kg<sup>−1</sup>. Even after the power density increases to 5216.89 W kg<sup>−1</sup>, an energy density of 52.17 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> still remains (Table S1). In addition, after the SEI and CEI formation in the first cycle, the CV curves of Sb||graphite full cell show excellent compatibility and reversibility with 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC+5 % FEC electrolyte (Figure <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0006">6c</a>). As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0006">6d</a>, <a href="#anie202313142-fig-0006">e</a>, the Sb||graphite full cell also exhibits superior rate performance with a capacity of 62.4, 57.9, 47.1, 38.3, 30.0, 20.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (based on the mass of graphite cathode) at current densities of 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0 and 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202313142-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2d79692c-161d-4927-93e1-469b2f14688a/anie202313142-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/2d79692c-161d-4927-93e1-469b2f14688a/anie202313142-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9be5651d-fdd0-4ace-9fc1-b91ecf6180d6/anie202313142-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2d79692c-161d-4927-93e1-469b2f14688a/anie202313142-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202313142-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202313142" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Electrochemical performance of the Sb||graphite full cell. (a) Schematic illustration of the Sb||graphite full cell during the charging process. (b) The comparison of cycling performance of the Sb||graphite full cell in 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC and 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC+5 % FEC electrolyte. (c) The CV curves of Sb||graphite cell in 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC+5 % FEC electrolyte with a scan rate of 0.5 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. (d) Rate performance and corresponding (e) charge/discharge curves of Sb||graphite full cell with 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>−PC+5 % FEC electrolyte at various current densities.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202313142-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202313142-sec-0003-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, the utilization of FEC additive greatly improves the reversible capacity and cycling performance of Sb||graphite Na−DIBs. For the Sb anode, adding FEC molecules facilitates the formation of a robust and stable NaF-rich SEI, effectively suppressing the decomposition of salts and solvents. Notably, the presence of FEC in the solvation sheath surrounding PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions promotes the anion intercalation into graphite cathodes as well as high-efficiency de-intercalation. Additionally, a robust fluorinated CEI film can be generated on the graphite surface, which effectively stabilizes graphite structure and significantly suppresses electrolyte oxidative decomposition and the expansion of graphitic layers. As a consequence, the formation of NaF-rich CEI and SEI, as well as distinctive solvation chemistry of fluorinated ligands, enabled an improved lifespan and Coulombic efficiency of Sb anodes and graphite cathodes, eventually ensuring long cycling stability of Sb||graphite full cell. The strategy proposed and demonstrated in this work sheds light on the dual effect of fluorinated molecules modification in the formation of an inorganic NaF-rich EEI on both cathode and anode, as well as changes the solvation behavior of anions, which provides a straightforward approach for building high-performance Na−DIBs and promotes their practical applications in future sustainable energy storage systems.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
